1986\n\nfW_.qA\nNational Aeronautics\nand Space Administration\nScientific and Technical\nInformation Branch\n1986\n\nPREFACE\n\nThe Jet Propulsion Laboratory in cooperation\nhosted the Sixth Annual TAE Users\'Conference\nPasadena California Conference Center.\n\nwith Goddard Space Flight Center\non October 8-10, 1986 at the\n\nThe purpose of the conference was to allow users to interchange\ninformation on\nthe use of TAE and its various applications\nas well as facilitate interaction\nbetween users and developers on the future of TAE.\nTo meet these intentions,\nthis year\'s conference offered an excellent balance of time for presentations,\ndemonstrations,\nspecial interest discussions\nand informal discussions.\nThe presentations and conversations\ndemonstrated\nthat many TAE users have the\nsame needs and goals.\nThefe are several TAE sites interested in developing\ndevice-independent\nimaging applications,\naccessing data bases, designing User\nInterface Management Systems (UIMS), executing TAE on PCs, creating\nprogramming tools and utilizing windowing systems.\nIt is hoped that this\nmeeting will result in solid work towards coordination between TAE sites and\nfuture enhancements to TAE.\n\nTAE Project\nNASA/Goddard\nAcknowledgement\n\ns:\n\nThe Transportable Applications\nExecutive (TAE) is being developed by the\nNASA/Goddard Space Flight Center and by Century Computing, Inc. The work is\nsponsored by the NASA\nInformation Systems Office Code (EI) of the Office of\nSpace Science and Applications\n(Code E) and the Office of Space Tracking and\nData Systems (Code T).\n\nProceedings from the\nSixth Annual TAE User\'s Conference\nOctober 8 - 10, 1986\nHosted by\nJet Propulsion Laboratory\nin cooperation\nwith\nGoddard Space Flight part\nof\na\nparameter,\nthe alias\ntext\xe2\x80\xa2 is substituted.\nEach\nuser\nwill\nhave\nhis own\nset of defined\naliases.\nAlias\ninformation\nwill\nbe make\nthe\nfile\neditable\nby EDT,\nwhich\ndoes\nnot handle\nrecords\nlonger\nthan\n255 characters,\nthe\nfile will\nconsist\nof pairs\nof records.\nThe\nfirst\nrecord\nin high\nlevel\nof\nproductivity\nwithin\nthe\n2GCHAS\nproject.\nFor\nthe\ndocuments,\nruptions\nthat\nthe\n\nprogrammer\nat\na to\nuse\nDEFCMD\n\nused\nVAX/VMS\nDCL\ncommands\navailable\ninside\nis\nhaving\nPDFs\nwhich\nallow\nvarious\neditors\ninside\n2GCHAS.\nThe\nDEFCMDs\nand\neditor\nPDFs\n\nthat\nthe\n2GCHAS\nspects,\nmaking\nsmoother\nfor\nthe\n\npaper\nintermeans\n\nand\nDCL\nenvironments\nthe\ntransition\nfrom\ndevelooer.\n121\n\nare\nthe\nsame\none\nenvironment\n\nin\nto\n\nSo\nuser\nbut\nto\n\n_GCHAS.\nto\nbe\nmeans\n\nmany\nthe\n\nreother\n\n2GCHAS\n\n2GCHAS\nproviding\n\n-\n\nA\n\nHigh\n\nProductivity\n\nSoftware\n\nchose\nto\nuse\nmessage\nonline\ndiagnostic\n\nhelo\nfiles\ninformation.\n\nDevelopment\n\nas a\nThe\n\nEnvironment\n\norimary\nsimple\n\nand\n\nmeans\nof\noowerful\n\nmechanisms\nof message\nhelp\nfiles\nand\nthe\nMSGBLD\nutility\ncontribute\nto\nproductivity\nin three\nways.\nThe\nfirst\nis that\nmessages\nand\ntheir\ncorresponding\nhelp\ncan\nbe grouped.\nHaving\nthe\nhelp\nfor\npossible\nmessages\nproduced\nby related\nsoftware\nunits\n-- a\nTAE\nfacility\n-- together\nin a single\nplace\nmakes\nit easier\nto\nget\nconsistency\nof\ninformation\nacross\nmessages.\nThe\nsecond\nis\nthat\nthe\n2GCHAS\nproject\nexperience\nindicates\nthat\nmessage\nkeys\ntend\nto\nget\nreused\nfor\nsimilar\nor\nidentical\nsituations.\nWhen\nthis\nhappens,\nthere\nis\nless\nhelp\ntext\nto generate\nand\nthe\nmessage\ntext\ncan\nbe\nuse_\nto\nprovide\noccurrance\nspecific\ninformation.\nThe\nthird\nproperty\nof message\nfiles\nwhich\nacts\nto\nreduce\neffort\nis\nthat\nthey\nare\nlocated\noutside\nof\nthe\nsoftware\nunits\nto\nwhich\nthey\napply.\nThis\nmeans\nthat\nthe\ncontinual\nactivity\nof\nmaking\nclarifications\nand\nadditions\nto message\nhelp\nnoes\nnot\na0oect\nthe\nsoftware\nunits\nthemselves.\nTAE\nfeatures\nhave\nsignificantly\nreduced\nthe\ntesting\neffort\nfor\n2GCHAS.\nFormal\ntesting,\nthe\nuse\nof\npredefined\nand\noocumented\ntest\nscenarios\ngenerating\noutput\nfor\ncomparison\nagainst\nexpected\nresults,\nis a 2GCHAS\nproject\nrequirement.\nThe\nconten\'ts\nc,f each\nnew\nsystem\ngeneration\nor\nbuild\nare\nquickly\ncataloguec\nwith\nscri0t\nfiles\ncontaining\nTUTOR\nand\nHELP\ncommands\nfor\neach\n2GCHAS\nservice.\nIf\nthe\nscript\ncompletes\nwithout\nstooping,\nthen\nall\nof\nthe\nservices\nare\npresent.\nA\nmissing\nservice\nstops\nthe\nscri ot\n-$MESSAGE\nis\nset\nto\n"ATTN ....\nallowing\nthe\ntester\nto\nlog\nthe\nmissing\nservice.\nThe\nscript\nfiles\nprovide\nfor\na level\nof\nspeed\nand\nrepeatability\nthat\na person\nat a terminal\nwith\na written\ntest\nscenario\ncan\nonly\ndream\nof\napproaching.\n2GCHAS\ntest\nprocedures\nfying\nand\nself\nreporting.\ndetermines\nwhether\nor\n\nhave\nnot\n\nbeen\ndesigned\nto\nbe both\nself\nBasically\nthis\nmeans\nthat\nthe\nit succeeded\nand\nthen\nreoorts\n\nveritest\nthe\n\nsuccess\nto the\ntest\nconductor.\nThe\neffort\nto\nperform\nand\ndocument\nformal\ntesting\nis reduced\nbecause\nof these\ntest\n0rocedures.\nIn addition\nto scripts,\ntests\nare\nimplemented\nas\nprocedures\nand\norocesses.\n$SFI,\nthe\nsuccess/fail\nindicator,\nis set\nby Ben Fahy, Robert Kaucic, and Yongmin\nDepartment\nof Electrical Engineering\nUniversity of Washington\nSeattle, Washington 98195\n\n179\n\nOF TAE\n\nKim\n\nPOTENTIAL\n\nMEDICAL\n\nJ. Ben Fahy,\n\nRobert\n\nAPPLICATIONS\n\nKaueic,\n\nand\n\nYongmin\n\nOF TAE\n\nKim\n\nDepartment\nof Electrical\nEngineering\nUniversity\nof Washington\nSeattle,\nWashington\n98195\n\nABSTRACT\n\nIn cooperation\nwith\nscientists\nin the University\nof Washington\nMedical\nSchool,\nwe have constructed\na microcomputer-based\nimage processing\nsystem\nfor\nquantitative\nmicroscopy,\ncalled DMDI,\nfor "Digital\nMicrodensitometer\n#1. _ In\norder\nto make DMD1 transportable\nto different\nhosts and\nimage\nprocessors,\nwe\nhave been investigating\nthe possibility\nof rewriting\nthe lower level portions\nof\nDMDI\nsoftware\nusing TAE libraries\nand subsystems.\nIf successful,\nwe hope\nto\nproduce\na newer version\nof DMDI,\ncalled\nDMD2, running\non an IBM PC/AT\nunder\nthe\nSCO XENIX\nSystem V operating\nsystem,\nusing any of seven target\nimage processors\navailable\nin our laboratory.\nFollowing\nthis implementation,\nwe will\ntransfer\ncopies of the system to other laboratories\nwith biomedical\nimaging\napplications.\nBy integrating\nthose applications\ninto DMD2,\nwe hope to eventually\nexpand\nour\nsystem\ninto\na low-cost\ngeneral\npurpose\nbiomedical\nimaging\nworkstation.\nThis\nworkstation\nwill be useful not only as a self-contained\ninstrument\nfor\nclinical\nor research\napplications,\nbut also as part of a large scale\nDigital\nImaging\nNetwork\nand Picture\nArchiving\nand Communication\nSystem,\n(DIN/PACS).\nWidespread\napplication\nof these\nTAE-based\nimage processing\nand analysis\nsystems\nshould\nfacilitate\nsoftware\nexchange\nand scientific\ncooperation\nnot only\nwi.thin\nthe\nmedical\ncommunity,\nbut between\nthe medical\nand remote\nsensing\ncommunities\nas\nwell.\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nQuantitative\nmicroscopy\nis an important\ntool for researchers\nand clinicians\nin various\nmedical\ndisciplines.\nIt is composed\nof two\nquite\ndifferent\nmethodologies:\nmorphometry,\nin which\nspatial\nproperties\nare\nmeasured,\nand\ndensitometry/fluorometry_\nwhich measures\nmass or activity.\nThe principles\nwhich\nunderlie\nspecific\ntechniques\nof either\nkind are well\nunderstood,\nand\nanalog\ninstruments\nranging\nin sophistication\nfrom conventional\nmicroscopes\nfitted\nwith\nphotomultiplier\ntubes\n(PMT)\nto scanning\nmicrodensitometers\nand\nflow\nmicrofluorometers\nhave\nemerged.\nUnfortunately,\nthese instruments\ntend to be\nspecialized\n(inflexible),\nare expensive,\nand are slow and difficult\nto interface\nto computers.\nAs a promising\nalternative,\na digital\ntechnique\nhas recently\narisen\nbased on interfacing\na camera\ndirectly\nto the microscope,\nand using image\nprocessing\noperations\nto analyze\nthe resultant\ndigitized\nimages.\n\n180\n\nIn Dec. 1982, we began a joint effort with researchers\nin the Department\nof\nPathology\nto develop\nour own image processing\nsystem,\nand apply\nit to the\nmeasurement\nof DNA content in hypertensive\nsmooth muscle cells.\nBy Dec.\n1984,\nthe prototype\nsystem,\nwhich we refer to as DMDI (Digital\nMicroDensitometer\n#1)\nwas completed,\nand it has been heavily utilized\nin running\nexperiments\nand\nanalyzing\nimages\nsince\nthat\ntime.\nNew algorithms\nand features\nhave\nbeen\ncontinuously\nincorporated\ninto the software [Nicholls\net al., 1985; Vinter\net\nal., 1985; Vinter\net al., 1986] with the result that new\napplications\nin\nautomated\ngrain counting,\nimmunocytochemistry,\nand other disciplines\ncan now be\ndeveloped\nas natural extensions\nto our existing system.\nThis expandability\nis a\nkey advantage\nof digital methods in quantitative\nmicroscopy,\nsince\nthe same\nbasic\nmethod\ncan be applied to any number\nof different\napplications.\nOther\nadvantages\nof DMDI include improvements\nin speed (200 cellular\nDNA measurements\nper\nhour vs. approximately\n40-50 per hour for conventional\nanalog\nmethods),\naccuracy,\nand the possibility\nof conducting\nsimultaneous\nanalysis\nof morphometry\nand densitometry.\nComparison\nof analog and digital systems\nso far has indicated\nthat\ndigital\nmethods for densitometry/fluorometry\nare at least as good as the\nbest analog devices\n[Vinter et al., 1985].\nWe are now at the stage with our system that it is appropriate\nto consider\nmaking\nDMD1\n(and\nits successor,\nDMD2)widely\navailable\nto other\nmedical\nresearchers\nby moving the software\nto a more accessible\ncombination\nof host\nand\nimage\nprocessor.\nIn its current\nimplementation,\nDMDI resides\non a custom\nMotorola\nMC68010-based\nhost microcomputer\nand uses a CAT 1600 image\nprocessing\nsubsystem\n(Digital\nGraphics,\nPalo Alto, CA). We are in the process\nof\ntransporting\nthe entire image processing\nand analysis\nsystem to an IBM PC/AT\nwith\nan ITI (Imaging\nTechnologies\nInc., Woburn,\nMA) FG-100-AT\nimage processor\nboard.\nDrivers\nfor the image processor under the SCO XENIX\nSystem V operating\nsystem\nhave\nbeen\nwritten,\nand\na majority of the DMDI\nsoftware\nhas\nbeen\nsuccessfully\nported.\nBecause\nour original system runs UNIX System V and\nthe\nsoftware\nwas written in a very layered\nfashion which isolated\ndevice-dependent\nportions\nof the code,\nother than writing the new drivers and emulating\nthe CAT\nimage\nprocessing\nfunctions,\ntransporting\nthe software\npackage\nhas been\nrelatively\nstraightforward.\nNow that we have copies of DMDI on at least two hardware\nconfigurations,\nwe\nface\na problem\nassociated\nwith maintaining\nsoftware\ncompatibility\nbetween\nthe\ntwo implementations.\nAs new applications\nare developed\non each\nsystem,\nthey\nshould\nbe ported\nover to the other as rapidly\nas possible_\nEvery\ntime\nan\napplication\ntakes advantage\nof the device-dependent\nfeatures\nof one system,\nit\nwill\nhave\nto be emulated\non the other.\nIf a third system is added,\nthen its\nhardware\nfeatures\nwill have to be emulated\non the other two, and it will in turn\nhave\nto emulate\ntheir special features.\nRapidly,\nas the number\nof different\nimage\nprocessors\nincreases,\nthis\nemulation\nstrategy\nis likely\nto become\noverwhelmed\nby the sheer number of combinations\nwhich would have to be managed.\nTherefore,\nwe are seeking\nto develop\nalternative\ntransportation\nstrategies\nnow,\nwhich\nwill lessen the difficulty\nin maintaining\nmany different\nimplementations\nof DMDI in the future.\nThe\nresearch\n\nissue of system transportability\nis being felt not\ngroup,\nbut is beginning\nto be recognized\nwithin\n\n181\n\nonly\nthe\n\nwithin our own\ngeneral\nmedical\n\nimage processing community as well. There are many different kinds of image\nprocessing systems being used in various clinicaland research laboratories.In\napplications including fluorescent microscopy, microdcnsitomctry, neurological\nmorphomctry, autoradiography, and others, hosts range primarily among\nVAXcs,\nLSIs, PDPs, Eclipses,\nand PC compatibles [Smith ct al.,1985, Ramm\net al.,1984,\nPuls et al., 1986]. The image processors commonly used are manufactured\nby\nvendors including Gould, IIS, Grinncl, MATROX,\nITI, Datacube, and others .In\nsome cases, such as in our laboratory, special-purpose image processors are\ndesigned and builtfrom scratch to mcct a particular application need. Other\ninstallationsmay have important peripherals available such as array or signal\nprocessors. Because nearly allof these systems have been developed separately,\non widely varying combinations of host and image processor, very little\nconstructive sharing has taken place between these research groups. Effectively,\nthis means that each of these efforts has reduplicated the others, leaving\nlittle\nopportunity for the development and implementation of more sophisticated\ntools.\nIn\n\nwhat\n\nfollows\n\nwe\n\nconsider\n\nthe\n\napplication\n\nof\n\nTAE\n\nto\n\nthis\n\ntransportability\nproblem\nin biomedical\nimage processing,\nand how we propose to\nuse TAE to make our own system,\nDMD2,\nmore widely available.\nWe are especially\ninterested\nin the development\nof a Biomedical\nVirtual Image\nProcessor\n(BVIP),\nalong the lines of the DMS subsystem\nof TAE. We will discuss briefly\nthe path we\nintend\nto take toward incorporating\nthe TAE structure\nwithin our system,\nits\npotential\nimplementation\non a range of seven image processors\nof widely-varying\narchitecture\nand capability,\nand the ramifications\nof these modifications\nto the\npractical\npossibility\nof creating\na general\npurpose\nbiomedical\nimaging\nworkstation.\nFinally,\nwe will consider\nthe use of such workstations\nin a largescale\nDigital\nImaging\nNetwork\nand Picture Archiving\n& Communication\nSystem\n(DIN/PACS).\nIf we are successful\nin propagating\na TAE-based\nDMD2 to this extent,\nit will open up new opportunities\nfor direct and effective\nsoftware\nexchange\nand\ncooperation.\n\nMETHODS\n\nDMD2 is nearly\ncomplete,\ni.e., porting DMD1 to the IBM AT host and ITI\nimage\nprocessor.\nThe next step is to implement\nDMD2 on up to six other\nimage\nprocessors\nin our lab, each of which will be discussed\nbriefly\nbelow.\nFor this\ntask,\nwe will replace portions of our system with the TAE Display\nManagement\nSubsystem\n(DMS) and refine\nDMD2\nand DMS as necessary\nto allow\nthe same\napplications\nprogram\nto run on any of seven\ndifferent\nimage\nprocessors.\nFollowing\nthe completion\nof this task,\nwe will integrate\nthe remainder\nof DMD2\ninto the TAE monitor structure,\ntaking advantage\nof TAE\'s built-in\nhelp, tutor,\nand other facilities.\nAs it becomes\nnecessary,\nwe will then be in a position\nto\nport DMD2\nto other operating\nsystems on which TAE is supported,\nand all new\nprograms\nwritten\nfor DMD2 can be created from the beginning\nwithin\nthe TAE\nprogramming\ncontext.\nIn Figs. I and 2 are presented simplified representationsof the DMDI\nand TAE software architectures,respectively.\nWe will compare the two systems in\na top to bottom fashion, noting both the obvious differences and some important\n\n182\n\nsimilarities\nas well.\nTo begin with,\nin TAE, the top level software\nmodule is\nthe TAE monitor,\nwhich initiates\nprocesses or TCL command\nlanguage\nprocedures,\nprovides\naccess to the help and menu and tutor facilities,\nand in many ways can\nmimic\nthe performance\nof a generic operating\nsystem.\nThere is no equivalent\nto\nthis module in DMDI. Instead, for most users, a pre-defined\nsequence\nof programs\nis executed,\nwhich\ntogether\nperform\na full\ndensitometry\noperation\n(from\ndigitization\nthrough\ndecalibration\nto cell identification\nand analysis).\nThis is\nfor the benefit\nof the medical\nresearchers\nand technicians,\nmany of whom\nare\nunfamiliar\nwith\ncomputers\nto the extent that any deviation\nfrom a fixed\nand\nrigid\npattern\nof interaction\nis considered\nundesirable.\nFor system\nand\napplication\nprogrammers,\nindividual\nroutines may be invoked\ndirectly\nusing UNIX.\nThus far,\nthis\ninterface\nhas proved adequate,\nbut as the\ngeneral\nlevel\nof\ncomputer\nexpertise\nwithin the biomedical\ncommunity\nimproves,\nand as DMD2 itself\nexpands\nto the point where the programmers\nthemselves\nwill require some sort of\na help facility,\nwe will need to turn to some sort of a monitor\nsuch\nas is\nprovided\nby TAE. In fact,\nas will be discussed\nbelow,\nwe eventually\nplan\nto\nimplement\na version of DMD2 which runs as an application\nunder TAE.\nBelow\nthe TAE\napplications\nprograms.\nhigher-level\nfunctions\nprograms\nfor:\n-\n\nmonitor,\nand as the top layer in our system,\nreside the\nIn DMD2 most of these routines\nare dedicated\nto the\nrequired\nfor quantitative\nmicroscopy.\nThis\nincludes\n\nsystem initialization\nand calibration\nimage digitization\nand image display (B/W, pseudo color)\nimage management\n(image handling and cataloging)\ninteractive\ndevice management\n(tree-structured\nmenu generation)\nhistogram\ngeneration\n(whole image or specified\nregion)\nimage contrast\nenhancement\n(lookup table manipulation)\nalgebraic\noperations\n(+,-,x,/)\ngeometric\noperations\n(translation,\nrotation,\nroam and zoom)\n2-D convolution\nfor spatial filtering\n(with region of interest)\n2-D Fast Fourier Transform\nvarious edge enhancement\nand boundary detection\nalgorithms\nuser manipulable\ncursors for region of interest\nanalysis\nimage intensity\nprofile\nalong any specified\nline\nthresholding\nfor object segmentation\nand selection\ndecalibration\nto correct uneven illumination\nin the microscope,\nand the camera\'s\nnonuniform\nphotometric\nsensitivity\ndensitometry\noperations\nfor quantitative\nmeasurements.\nautomatic\nboundary\ndetection\nalgorithms\nautomatic\nmorphometry\nwith densitometry\ncomplexity\nanalysis\n\nThe total\nprogramming\neffort\nfor our system in this regard\nthus far is\napproximately\n4 man years. Each of these routines\nmay be executed\nas stand-alone\nprocedures\nor as part of a larger densitometry\nprogram\nchain.\nMoved into ,TAE,\nthis\nchain\ncould be implemented\nvery easily,\nsimply by using\na TCL command\nprocedure\nto invoke the individual\napplications\nprograms\none after the other.\nBelow\nindependent\n\nthe applications\nlayer\nlibrary of standardized\n\nin TAE is DMS,\nimage processing\n\n183\n\nwhich provides\na devicefunctions.\nIt is composed\n\nof\n\nan X-layer\nwhich is written independent\nof the particular\nhardware\ndevice\nbeing\nused,\nand a D-layer,\nwhich\nis device-dependent.\nIn the position\ncorresponding\nto DMS in DMD2 is the Firmware\nExtension\nLayer,\ncreated\nto expand\nthe capabilities\nof CAT firmware\ncommands.\nIt is important\nto appreciate\nthat in\nboth systems,\nthese layers (DMS and the Firmware\nExtension\nLayer) act as the\non_\ninterface\nbetween\nthe applications\nprograms and the lower-level\nroutines\nbelow.\nThis common feature\nof the two designs is what will make replacement\nof\nthe Firmware\nExtension\nLayer with DMS straightforward.\nBelow DMS in TAE is the vendor interface\nlayer, which corresponds\nto the\nFirmware\nInterface\nLayer in DMD2.\nEach of these layers is designed\nto contain\nsoftware\nfunctions\ncompletely\nspecific\nto the\nparticular\nimage\nprocessor\nsupported.\nAt the lowest\nlevel of either software\nstructure\nare the device\ndrivers,\nreferred to as the Physical\nInterface\nLayer in DMD2. As has previously\nbeen mentioned,\nthese have already\nbeen written\nfor the IBM AT running SCO XENIX\nSystem\nV. Separate drivers are used to perform memory-mapped\nI/O,\nmanage\nI/O\nchannels,\nand service\ninterrupts.\nIn most image\nprocessing\nsystems,\nframe\nbuffers\nare directly\nmemory-mapped,\nimage processor\nregisters\nare mapped either\nthrough memory or through I/O channels,\nand interactive\ndevices\nsuch as mouses,\ntrackballs,\nor bit-pads are best implemented\nusing interrupt\nservice\nroutines.\nIn order to provide a comprehensive\nand thorough\ntesting\nground for the\nDMS implementation\nin our system,\nwe intend to create up to seven versions\nof\nthe device-dependent\nD-layer,\none for each image processor\nwhich is available\nin\nour lab. These image processors\nrange widely in capability:\ndifferences\ninclude\nthe number of bits per pixel in the frame buffer (gray-scale\nresolution),\nwidth\nof the look-up tables,\nand prescence\nor absence of graphics overlays,\nhardware\ncursor support, display zoom, pan, and scroll, coprocessors,\nand dedicated\nimage\noperations\nhardware,\ne.g., histogram\ngeneration,\nimage arithmetic,,\nand image\nfiltering.\nThe Digital Graphics\nSystems CAT-1600\ngraphics\nboard features\nreal-time\ndigitizing,\nzoom, pan and scroll,\nand dedicated\ngraphics and image processing\ncommands\nimplemented\nin an Intel 8086-based\nsubsystem.\nOur implementation\nuses\none\n512 x 512 x S-bit bit frame buffer,\nwhich is directly\nmemory-mapped\nin the\nhost address space over an IEEE 696 (S-100) bus. Communication\nwith the host is\nfacilitated\nby 4 16-bit I/O ports in the IEEE 696 bus: a data port,\ncommand\nport,\nreset port, and status port. Three independent\nlook-up tables of 8 bits\neach are assigned to red, green,\nand blue, enabling\npseudocolor\noptions. There\nis no dedicated\nhardware\nsupport for the cursor,\nhowever,\na cursor is emulated\nin the firmware\npackage by overwriting\nthe frame buffer\nto display the cursor,\nand restoring\nthe data when the cursor is moved.\nThe ITI FG-100-AT\nimage processing\nboard resides on the IBM AT bus and\ncontains\n16 I/O channel-mapped\nregisters to initiate\ncommands\nand receive status\ninformation.\nThe 512 x 512 x 12-bit frame buffer\nis directly\nmemory-mapped\ninto\nthe host\naddress space.\nThree 4096 x 8-bit look-up tables\nare provided\nfor\npseudo\ncolor or even true color operation.\nLike the CAT, real-time\ndigitization,\nzoom,\npan and scroll are supported\nin hardware.\nThere is no image processor.\nA\nfeedback\nloop arithmetic\nunit is provided,\nhowever,\nwhereby\n6-bit images may be\nadded,\nsubtracted,\nmultiplied\nor divided in one frame display\ntime. In our\nstandard\noperations,\nwe use 8 bits of each pixel for gray level,\n3 bits\nfor\n\n184\n\ngraphics, and 1 bit for the cursor. Thereis no hardware cursor support, which\nagain must be emulatedby writing into the frame buffer\'s cursor plane.\nWe have had somesuccess\nalready in moving portions of DMDI to an IBM\nProfessional Graphics Adaptor (PGA)in an IBM AT environment. This raster\ngraphics board consistsof a 640 x 480 x 8-bit frame buffer, three 256 x 4-bit\nlook-up\ntables,\nand an Intel 8086 based coprocessor\nwhich\nsupports\nthreedimensional\ngraphics\nand several international\nstandard graphics\npackages\nsuch\nas the Graphical\nKernel System (GKS). Communication\nbetween\nthe host and the PGA\nis accomplished\nvia memory-mapped\ncommand,\ndata and error buffers\nand additional\nregisters.\nThere is no support for hardware\nzoom, pan, or scroll, and the cursor\nmust be emulated.\nThis system is our lowest capability\nsystem.\nOn the other\nside of the spectrum is a Gould\nIP8400\nimage\nprocessing\nsystem,\nwhich\nsupports\nmany image analysis/processing\nfeatures\nin hardware,\nresulting\nin high performance.\nThe Gould IP8400 is equipped\nwith three 512 x 512\nx 8-bit frame buffers,\na video output controller,\na pipelined\nhigh-throughput\ndigital\nvideo processor,\nand a library of image processing\nsoftware\nfrom Gould.\nIts host computer\nis a MicroVAX\nII, which is networked\nthrough\nDECNET\nto other\ncomputers.\nIn our initial implementation\nof DMD2, we will run the UNIX operating\nsystem,\nalthough\nlater versions in which DMD2 runs as an application\nunder\nTAE\ncould be implemented\nunder VMS.\nThe TISDB board is a Texas Instruments\nSoftware\nDevelopment\nBoard based\non the TMS 34010 Graphics System Processor (GSP) chip. The TISDB has one 512 x\n512 x 4-bit frame buffer.\nIt has a look-up table scheme based on their palette\nchip which allows resetting\nof the three 16 x 4 bit look-up tables line by line.\nWhile\nthis board does not have the gray-scale\nresolution\nto be useful for most\nimage\nprocessing\napplications,\nit has proved\na useful\ntool for gaining\nfamiliarity\nwith the powerful\nGSP chip, and can similarly\nprovide a useful test\nof DMS. The GSP is a fully programmable\n32-bit graphics\nprocessor,\nwith special\nhardware\nfeatures\nsuch as a 256-byte\ninstruction\ncache and block\ndata move\nfacility,\nwhich\nmake\nit very effective\nfor some image\nprocessing\noperations\n[Guttag\net al., 1986]. Using a C compiler and loader,\nwe have implemented\na\nnumber\nof demonstration\nprograms\nfor the GSP, including\nzooming,\nconvolution,\nlook-up table manipulations,\ncursor,\nand menu support.\nWe have also written\na\nresident\nmonitor for the GSP, which loops until given a command\nfrom the host to\nexecute\na local program.\nUpon completion,\nall programs\nreturn to the monitor.\nThis interface\nis implemented\nboth in DOS and XENIX.\nWe are in the process now of developing\na sixth image\nprocessor,\nalso\nbased\non the GSP, but with much greater capability\nand gray-scale\nresolution.\nThis image processor,\nwhich we will refer to as UWGSPI,\nconsists\nof two boards\nwhich reside completely\nwithin the IBM AT, containing\nfive major sections;\nthe\ngraphics\nprocessor,\nframe buffer,\nvideo display,\nzoom hardware,\nand signal\nprocessor\n[Chauvin\net al., 1986]. It contains four 512 x 512 x 8-bit\nframe\nbuffers,\nplus four separate graphics\noverlay planes,\none of which is allocated\nto the cursor.\nThere are three 4096 x 8-bit look-up tables for red, green, and\nblue output.\nIndependent\nvertical\nand horizontal\nzoom is supported\nin hardware,\nas are pan and scroll.\nA separate\nsignal processing\ncoprocessor\nis provided\nbased on the TMS 32020 Digital\nSignal Processor (DSP) chip.\n\n185\n\nThe lastimage processor to be used is also in the development stage.It\nis called UWIPI, and is designed around a special-purpose high speed (30\nMBytcs/scc) image bus called the IBUS. It features an expandable central frame\nbuffer which currently contains 4 512 x 512 x 12-bit frames. The display\nand\nprocessor is based on the Hitachi HD63484 Advanced CRT Controller (ACRTC),\nprovides independent x and y zoom, three 4096 x 8-bit look up tables for\nred, green, and blue, hardware support for pan, scroll,\ncursor movement, and\nvarious graphics and annotation functions. Other key modules on the IBUS includc\na pipclined rcconfigurablc convolvcr, arithmetic and logic unit, look-up table\ntransformer and histogram generator, and host interface buffer. Region-ofinterest(ROI) operations are implemented in hardware.\nTo implcmcnt DMD2\non such a wide range of image processors, we will\nbegin by writing the DMS D-layer for each processor, using the D-layer provided\nfor the IIS Model 75 as an example. The other portions of DIVlS, for example the\nXD, DM,\nDT, and XL\nsubroutine packages, should port over in a fairly\nstraightforward manner. Because our medical applications do not at this timc\ninvolve multispectral image analysis, we will delay supporting the image\nconfiguration utility,\nuntil a specificnccd for multispcctralclassification\nin\nradiology using images from multiple imaging modalitics arises. With DIVlS\navailable for each several image processors, we will rewrite the Firmware\nExtension Layer of DMD2\nso that the functions called by that layer are\nimplemented using callsto DMS. This will provide the quickest port of DMD2\npossibleconsistentwith the TAE architecture, the Application Layer of DMD2,\nas\nwhich was builtdirectlyon itsFirmware Extension Layer, should be immediately\ntransportablefrom that point on.\nMost DD routines\nwill be fairly\nroutine\nto implement.\nOf those routines\nexpected\nto provide\ndifficulty,\nmost are related\neither\nto FORTRAN77\nconversions\nor else to peculiarities\nof the IIS image processor\nwhich\nmade their\nway into\nDD. The\nDDCRDF\nand DDZMRN\nroutines\nexemplify\nspecial\ncases in which\ndifferent\nimage\nprocessors\nmay have difficulty\nin implementing\ndifferent\nfunctions.\nFor\nexample,\nDDCRDF\nis for the most part straightforward,\nexcept that it allows the\npossibility\nof a cursor\nBLINK\nattribute.\nWhile this could be supported\nfairly\neasily\non either UWGSP1 or UWlPI,\nsupporting\nthis feature\non other\nprocessors\n(without\nany dedicated\ncursor\nhardware)\nis probably\nmore trouble\nthan\nit is\nworth.\nIn the case of the DDZMRN\nfunction,\nthe problem\nis that the function\nis\nambiguous\nfor image\nprocessors\nwhich support\nindependent\nx and\ny zoom,\nor\narbitrary-size\nzoom, such as UWGSP1 or the TISDB.\nIt is important\nto note that DD in its current implementation\nleaves out\nmany\nhardware\nfeatures\nof our in-house\nimage processors\nwhich\ncan\nbe quite\nimportant\nto the efficiency\nof the running\nsystem.\nFor example,\nthe\nDevice\nCharacteristics\nMask of the DMS Display\nDevice\nTable contains\nonly\ng hardware\ncharacteristics\nwhich\nare checked\nfor existence\nthus\nfar:\nhardware\nzoom,\nhistogram\ngenerator,\nsplit screen,\nimage shift,\nscale on input, look-up\ntable\nbypass,\nalpha generator,\nand keypad\nbuttons.\nClearly,\nthese features\nhave been\nsingled\nout with a specific\nimage processor\nin mind. But almost certainly\nin our\nimplementation,\nin order to achieve\nmaximum\nefficiency,\nwe will also have\nto\ninclude\ncharacteristic\nflags for:\n\n186\n\n-\n\nindependent\nX and Y zoom (ITI, UWIP1, UWGSPI,\nhardware\nconvolver\n(UWIPI,\nUWGSPI)\narray or signal processor\n(UWGSP1)\narithmetic\nunit (ITI, UWIPI, UWGSPI, TISDB)\n\nTISDB)\n\nThese\ncapabilities\nwill have to be incorporated\ninto the system model of image\nprocessing\ncharacteristics\nin order to fully take advantage\nof the hardware\navailable.\nIn turn,\nnew routines should be added to DD and XD to allow\nthese\nfunctions\nto be callable\nfrom\napplications\nprograms.\nAnother\nimportant\nconsideration\nwhich\nDMS currently\nseems to leave unresolved\nis the nature\nof\ncommunication\nwith the image processor.\nDoes it reside on a separate\nbus? Are\nframe buffers\ndirectly\nmemory-mapped,\nwhich greatly simplifies\nimage loading,\nor\nis a more complicated\ninterface\nrequired?\nShould interrupt\nroutines\nbe used as\nan integral\npart of the communication\nstrategy,\nor is a polling or master/slave\ncommunication\nmodel sufficient7\nWith the wide range of image processors\navailable\nin our laboratory\nto\nexperiment\non, we expect that we will experience\na great many difficulties\nin\nadapting\na consistent\nDNIS interface\nto each image processor.\nOn the other hand,\nit is our hope that this experience\nwill prove very profitable\nin that solutions\nto these problems\ninevitably\nwill be worked out, and a more sophisticated\nmodel\nof image processor capability\nthan is presently\noutlined\nin DlVlS may\nresult.\nIt\nshould\nbe noted\nthat as image processing\nhardware\ncontinues\nto improve\nover\ntime,\nthe model will continually\nhave to be updated to correspond\nto the new\nstate\nof the art: the creation\nof a "virtual image processor," then,\nwhich is\nthe fundamental\ngoal of DMS, will indefinitely\nremain a very dynamic\nprocess. It\nis our feeling\nthat the virtual processor model will be kept best up to date\nby\ncontinually\nsubjecting\nthe model to new and widely-varying\ncapability\nimage\nprocessors,\nsuch\nas we will be attempting\nto do in the case of our widespread\nimplementation\nof DMD2.\n\nDISCUSSION\n\nThe previous\nsection has outlined\nthe method by which the DMS subsystem\nof TAE may be used to aid in the portability\nof our digital\nmicrodensitometer\nto\na wide variety of image processors.\nThe immediate\nbeneficial\neffects\nof such an\nexercise\nare (1) to achieve\nthe port itself,\nand (2) to improve the\nvirtual\nimage\nprocessor\nmodel\nof DMS to include\na more comprehensive\nlist of image\nprocessor\nfunctions.\nIn addition\nto these immediate\nbenefits,\nhowever,\nthere are\nother longer-term\nadvantages\nwhich are also important\nto consider.\nEither after or in parallel\nwith the implementation\nof DMS in DMD2,\nwe\nwill\nturn to the inclusion\nof the DMD2 application\nprograms\nwithin\nthe general\nframework\nof TAE.\nThis would most likely be attempted\nfirst for the IBM AT/ITIbased\nsystem.\nBesides\ncreating\nTAE format help files for the programs,\nsome\nrestructuring\nmight prove necessary\nbecause\nin DMD2,\nmany function\noptions\nare\ncurrently\nselected\nvia the cursor on a display-based\nmenu,\nas opposed to on a\nseparate\nterminal.\nIt should be noted,\nhowever,\nthat it is likely that\nsuch\nrestructuring\nwill only have to be carried out once, since after menus are moved\nto a separate\nterminal,\nthe rest of the TAE application\nprogram\ninteraction\n\n187\n\nshould\nbe dependent\nprocessor\nchosen.\n\nonly\n\non the\n\nIBM AT host,\n\nand not on the\n\nparticular\n\nimage\n\nWith\nDMD2\navailable\nas an application\npackage\nunder\nTAE,\nit should\nbecome\npossible\nto port\nDMD2\nto other operating\nsystems\non which\nTAE\nis\nsupported,\ne.g., the VMS operating\nsystem available\non our MicroVAX\nII. Wc will\nproceed\nto integrate\nDMD2 with other biomedical\nimage processing\nsystems\nand\napplications\nso as to fill out its overall\nfunctionality,\napproaching\nthe\ncreation\nof a general purpose\nbiomedical\nimaging\nworkstation.\nSince\neach\ninstallation\nwill be based on the TAE structure\nand libraries,\nit should be much\neasier\nto share software\nbetween\nusers of this system,\nas well as with\nthe\ninvestigators\nof completely\ndifferent\nfields of endeavor\nin the NASA\nremote\nsensing community.\nAs a final example of the utility\nof this approach,\nwe consider\nthe\napplication\nof the resultant\nworkstation\nto a large\nscale\nDigital\nImaging\nNetwork\nand Picture Archiving\nand Communications\nSystem (DIN/PACS)\n[Alzner\nal., 1986;\nParrish\net al., 1986]. The DIN/PACS\nis being be designed\nto aid\nradiologists\nin interpreting\nimages and associated\ndata for medical\ndiagnosis,\nand will provide for:\n- image capture by the system\n- storage of images\n- image retrieval\nfor diagnostic\nand display purposes\n-image\nmanipulation,\narrangement\nand enhancement\ndiagnostic\nviewing\n- entry and retrieval\nof clinical\ndata\n- diagnostic\nreport generation\n- indexed retrieval\nand statistical\nanalysis\nfor research\n- medical education\nactivities\n- radiology\ndepartment\nmanagement\n\nduring\n\npurposes\n\nThe fundamental\nadvantage\nof DIN/PACS\nover the present\nimage management\napproach\nused in a typical radiology\ndepartment\nis that it will allow physicians\nand\nradiologists\nto combine\nand analyze\nsimultaneously\nall\navailable\ninformation\non a patient,\nespecially\nincluding\ninternal\nimages produced\nby such\nimaging\nmodalities\nas Computer\nAided\nTomography\n(CAT),\nMagnetic\nResonance\nImaging\n(MRI),\nDigital\nSubtraction\nAngiography\n(DSA),\nPositron\nEmission\nTomography\n(PET), Ultrasound,\nand digitized\nX-Ray film. Each modality\npresents a\ndifferent\nview\nof the internal\nstate of the body,\nand by combining\nthe\ninformation\nobtained\nfrom all modalities,\nit may be possible\nto significantly\nincrease\nthe effectiveness\nof noninvasive\nradiological\ndiagnosis.\nBecause\nof the wide ranges of image processing\ncapability\nwhich\nis\nexpected\nto be used in DIN/PACS,\nit would be advantageous\nif the software\nused\nto develop\nand manage DIN/PACS\nimage processors\ncontained\nan effective\nvirtual\nimage processor model such as DMS may eventually\nprovide.\nFurther,\nit would be\nhighly\ndesirable to have some means of facilitating\ncommunication,\nexchange\nof\nsoftware\nand ideas,\nand general cooperation\nbetween\nthe propagators\nof DIN/PACS\nand the general image processing\ncommunity.\nTherefore,\nif we are successful\nin\ndeveloping\nthe biomedical\nimaging workstation\nin a timely\nfashion,\nwe will\nattempt\nto incorporate\nit into DIN/PACS,\nproviding\na TAE link between\nmedical\n\n188\n\net\n\nimaging\n\nand\n\nthe remote\n\nsensing\n\ncommunities.\n\nSUMMARY\n\nBiomedical\nimage\nprocessing\napplications\nhave been developed\nrelatively\nrecently\ncompared\nto applications\nin remote sensing.\nAs a consequence,\nmany\nbiomedical\nlaboratories\nand clinical\ninstallations\nare just now beginning\nto\nmeet\nhead-on\nthe problem of software\nportability\nwhich TAE was designed\nto\ncombat.\nIn our own laboratory,\nwe have developed\na microcomputer-based\nimage\nprocessing\nsystem for quantitative\nmicroscopy,\nwhich we would like to port to\nother\nhosts and image processors\nin order to expand its capabilities\nto that of\na biomedical\nimaging\nworkstation.\nBecause of the wide range of image processors\navailable\nto us, we have decided to replace the image\nprocessor\ninterface\nportion of our system with the DblS subsystem,\nthereby forcing\nthe development\nof\na Biomedical\nVirtual Image Processor\nwhich should greatly aid portability\nwithin\nthe biomedical\ncommunity.\nAs part of this process, DMS will be severely\ntested,\nrefined\nand enhanced.\nAs the workstation\ndevelops,\nit will be possible\nto\nincorporate\nit within a large scale DIN/PACS.\nBy using TAE as an integral\npart\nof these\nmedical\nimage\nprocessing\nsystems,\ngeneral\nsoftware\nexchange\nand\nscientific\ncooperation\nwill\nbe facilitated\nbetween\nthe medical\nand remote\nsensing image processing\ncommunities.\n\nREFERENCES\nAlzner,\nE., and Murphy,\nL., "The ACR-NEMA\ndigital\nimaging\nand Imaging,\nInc.\n\nand\n\nSerge\n\nEXECUTIVE\nMasse\n\nGlobal Imaging introduced\nan interactive\nimage processing\nsystem in\n1985, featuring\nthe Global\nApplications\nExecutive\n(GAE) which is a\nmodified\nTAE environment.\nThe executive\nplus a large variety\nof image\nprocessing\nfunctions,\nknown\ncommercially\nas the System 9000, are designed\nto operate on the Hewlett-Packard\nfamily\nof Unix computers.\nBeacause\nthe\nUS Navy has chosen the Hewlett-Packard\nas its standard\ndesktop\ncomputer\n(NSDTC),\nthe System 9000 has found\neasy acceptance\nfor naval image\nprocessing\napplications.\nIn 1986, Global\nhas installed\nits systems at the\nUS Naval Academy,\nthe Naval Research\nLaboratory\nand the Naval\nOceanographic\nFacility.\nOther naval installations\nare currently\nconsidering\nacquisition\nof this capability.\nThe Department\nof Oceanography\nat the Naval Academy,\nAnnapolis,\nMaryland,\nhas installed\nan NSDTC with an image processing\nupgrade\nprovided\nby Global Imaging.\nThis interactive\ndigital\nimage processing\nworkstation\nis used by the midshipmen\nand staff for training\nand research\nin remote\nsensing oceanography.\nThe turn-key\nsystem provides\nthe capability\nto\nprocess imagery\nfrom commonly\nused earth observation\nspacecraft,\nin\nconjunction\nwith in situ data sets. This marks the first time an\nundergraduate\noceanographic\ncurriculum\noffers\nsuch an advanced\ncapability.\nThe Acoustics\nGroup\nat the Naval Research\nLaboratory,\nWashington,\nhas acquired\nits first System 9000 to interactively\nprocess ocean acoustic\ndata gathered\nby shipboard\nsensors.\nThe vertical\nacoustic\nprofiles\nhad\nbeen processed\nup to now by a laborious\nbatch process.\nThe ease of user\ninteraction\nwill permit\nfaster analysis\nof the raw data.\n\nDC\n\nSeveral naval installations\nare currently\nworking\non the Tactical\nEnvironmental\nSupport\nSystem (TESS).\nThis shipboard\ncomputer\nsystem is\ntasked\nwith consolidating\noceanic\nand atmospheric\ninformation\nto aid\ntactical\ndecisions.\nThe first and second generations\nof TESS are based on\nthe NSDTC hardware,\nwhile the third generation\nhardware\nis still under\nconsideration.\nThe Naval Oceanographic\nFacility\nin Bay St. Louis,\nMississipi\nhas recently\nacquired\na System 9000 to provide\na TESS 2\nprototype\nwith image processing\ncapabilities.\nThis will permit\nmerging\nof conventional\ndata with polar orbiting\nspacecraft\nimagery.\nGlobal Imaging is proposing\nto utilize GAE as the user interface\nfor\nthe third generation\nof TESS.\nIn this system,\neach shipboard\ncomputer\nrequires\nfive workstations,\nfour of which must be capable\nof interactive\nimage processing.\nThe imagery\nwill be acquired\nin real-time\nfrom the\nNOAA, DMSP and NROSS polar orbiting\nsatellites.\nIn addition,\na vast array\nof climatological,\ngeophysical\nand recent\nenvironmental\ndata must be\nanalyzed\nto provide\ntactical\ndecision\naids for ship and aircraft\noperations\nat sea. This complex\nprocessing\nenvironment\ncan be greatly\nsimplified\nby providing\nthe operators\nwith the tutor,\nmenu and help\nfeatures\nof GAE.\n\n194\n\nHardware\nThe current System 9000 is based on the Hewlett-Packard\nSeries 300\nand 500 high-performance\n32-bit processor\n(CPU), with a direct address\nrange of 500 Megabytes.\nA separate\ninput/output\nprocessor (IOP) frees the\nCPU from functions\nassociated\nwith direct memory access by peripherals\nsuch as disk drives and displays.\nThe modular design of this computer\npermits\nmultiple\nCPUs and IOPs to reside on the same bus to provide\nincreased\nperformance\nwhen necessary. The computer\nconfiguration,\nincludes\na central\nprocessor\nunit (CPU), 4 Megabytes\nof solid state memory, and one\nIOP capable\nof handling\ndirect\nmemory access (DMA) transactions\non 8\nindependent\nchannels.\nThe new Series 800 processor,\nbased on reduced\ninstruction\nset (RISC) architecture,\ndelivers\n4.5 MIP performance\nwhile\nretaining\nsoftware\ncompatibility\nwith the previous\ngeneration\nprocessors.\nMass storage is provided\nby fixed or removeable\nmedia disk drives.\nThe Hewlett-Packard\n7900 series drives combine\nWinchester\ntechnology,\na\nseparate\nHP 9144 cartridge\ntape drive supplying\ncost effective\nbackup\nand\nuser I/O, and a sophisticated\nmicroprocessor-based\ncontroller\nto manage\nboth storage components,\nall integrated\ninto a single compact\npackage.\nA\n16 ms. average\naccess time and a data transfer\nrate of up to 1 Megabyte\nper second make these drives among the highest\nperforming\nmass storage\ndevices\navailable.\nThe Metheus\nOmega 3610 display controller\nis used to drive\nthe color\nCRT display.\nThe controller\nmemory\nis configured\nto hold 1024X1280X32-bit\nimages. These are displayed\nat 60 Hz non-interlaced\nrefresh\nrate using\nbright\ncolor monitors.\nThe custom bit-slice\nprocessor contained\nin the\nOmega 3610, with a cycle time of 167 nanoseconds,\ncan flash-fill\nrectangles\nat 160 million\npixels per second.\nThe display\ncontroller\ncommunicates\nwith the HP 9000 via a 16-bit\nparallel interface,\nusing a simple byte-oriented\nprotocol.\nThe command\nstructure\nminimizes\nthe the number\nof bytes which must be transferred\nfrom\nthe host to perform\ntime-critical\ntasks. A comprehensive\ninstruction\nset\n(Table\n1.) simplifies\nthe design of host computer\nsoftware\nand contributes\nto efficient\noperation.\nThe programmable\ncursor allows any size cross-hair\ncursor\nto be\nspecified.\nA set of 122 characters\n(8x16 pixels)\nis resident\nin ROM and is\nloaded\ninto a special character\nRAM at power up. Space is provided\nfor\nasecond\ncharacter\nset as well. Either character\nset may be chosen by the\nuser. Instructions\nare provided\nfor scaling character\nsize and specifying\ncharacter\nspacing.\nSoftware\nThe HP-UX\nOperating\nsystem, a licensed\nand supported\nversion of\nAT&T\'s\nSystem V, provides\na productive\nenvironment\nfor the development\nexecution\nof image processing\nsoftware.\nProgrammers\nandusers\nmay easily\nshare and reuse files and tools without\nsacrificing\nsystem security\nand\nreliability.\nThe HP-UX\noperating\nsystem features\nmulti-user\ncapability,\nmultitasking\ncapability,\nvirtual\nmemory for code and data, and engineering\ntools such as data base management\nand data communications.\n\n195\n\nand\n\nThe workstation\nprovides\nthe Global\nApplications\nExecutive,\nwhich\nstandardizes\nthe link between\nthe user and applications\nprograms\nunder\nthe UNIX operating\nsystem,. The user can operate\nthe system in three\nmodes. In the menu mode, the user is asked to make a selection\nfrom a list\nof menus and applications.\nIn the command\nmode, the user communicates\nwith\nthe system via simple English-like\ncommands.\nFinally,\nin tutor mode, the\nuser is prompted for all parameters\nwhich must be supplied\nto a program.\nIn both the command\nand tutor modes, the applications\nexecutive\nchecks\nthat all parameters\nentered by the user lie within\nthe correct\nrange. In\ncommand\nmode, if any parameter\nlies outside\na valid range,\nthe user is\nasked to re-enter the command;\nin tutor mode, he is re-prompted\nfor the\nparameter.\nParameters\nmay be optional\nor mandatory.\nOptional\nparameters\nnot specified\nby the user are assigned\ndefault\nvalues by the executive.\nHelp files, which display\ninformation\non the operation\nof the menu,\ncommand,\nand tutor mode as well as on the operation\nof all application\nfunctions,\nare available.\nA Programmer\'s\nSubroutine\nPackage was provided\nto accelerate\napplications\nsoftware\ndevelopment.\nThis package\ncontains\nFORTRAN\ncallable\nI/O routines for reading data from existing images as well as creating\nnew\nimages. Images created using this subroutine\npackage\nare automatically\ncataloged\nand can contain any number of lines and samples with up to 64\nbands. Image data can be any one of five types ranging\nfrom one byte per\npixel, to 64-bit real values per pixel.\nA sophisticated\nGraphics Software\nPackage (GSP) has been written\nto\nsupport the Omega 500 Display Controller.\nAt logon, the GSP assigns a\ndisplay, if one is available\nto each user. The user can operate in one of\nthree modes. In the first mode, the display is divided\ninto four 8-bit\nimage planes; in the second mode, three 8-bit image planes and one\ngraphics\noverlays\nare enabled;\nand in the third mode, two 8-bit image\nplanes and two graphics\noverlays\nare used. The GSP keeps track of the\ncurrent\ndisplay mode, active image and graphic overlay\nplanes, and active\nmonochrome\nor pseudocolor\nlook-up tables. Up to 32 pseudocolor\nand 32\nmonochrome\nlook-up tables can be stored and easily recalled.\nThe applications\nsoftware\nincludes\nprograms\nto perform\ngeometric\ncorrection,\nearth location,\nand registration\nof remotely\nsensed data.\nThese programs handle\nimagery\nfrom the Advanced\nVery High Resolution\nRadiometer\n(AVHRR),\nthe Coastal Zone Color Scanner\n(CZCS), the\nMultispectral\nScanner (MSS), the Scanning\nMultichannel\nMicrowave\nRadiometer\n(SMMR), and the Visual\nand Infrared\nSpin Scan Radiometer\n(VISSR).\nOther programs\npermit displaying\nmonochrome\nand true-color\nimages. Line graphics\ncan be overlayed\nonto the displayed\nimage in\ndifferent\ncolors. Interactive\nmanipulation\nof these images\nis possible via\na digital\ntablet provided.\nInteractive\nfunction\ninclude\npanning,\nhistogram\nnormalization\nand pseudocolor\nmanipulation.\n\n196\n\nN87-23168\n\nAN OVERVIEW\n\nOF TIlE LAND ANALYSIS\n\nYun-Chi Lu\nNASA/GSFC\n\n197\n\nSYSTEM (LAS)\n\nLAND Technology,\nEROS\nI.\nThe\n\nData\n\nCenter\n\nInc.\nSioux\n\nFalls,\n\nSD\n\nIntroduction\nLAS display\n\nmodules\n\nwere\n\ndeveloped\n\nto\n\nallow\n\na user\n\nto\n\ninteract-\n\nively display,\nmanipulate,\nand store image and image related\ndata.\nTo help accomplish\nthis task, these modules\nutilize\nthe Transportable\nApplications\nExecutive\nand the Display\nManagement\nSystem\nsoftware\nto\ninteract\nwith the user and the display\ndevice.\nFigure\n1 shows how\nthese components\nrelate\nto one another\nand the shaded\nbox is\nwhere\nthe major focus of this discussion\nwill be.\nFirst,\nthe basic\ncharacteristics\nof a display\nwill be outlined;\nsome of the major modifications\nand additions\nmade to the display\nmanagement\nsoftware\nwill\nbe discussed\nnext; finally,\nall available\nLAS display\nmodules\nalong\nwith a short description\nof each will be listed.\nII.\n\nBasic\n\nDisplay\n\nCharacteristics\n\nThe LAS display\nmodules\ndo not interact\nwith one specific\ntype of\ndevice,\nbut they do assume\nthe display\nhas several\nbasic characteristics.\nThey are: (I) a set of memory\nplanes\nfor storing\nimage data,\n(2) a set of graphics\nplanes\nfor storing\ngraphics\ndata such as text,\nlinework,\nhistograms\nand tick marks,\n(3) a look-up\ntable for mapping\nimage data, and (4) a pointing\ndevice\nfor user interaction\nwith the\ndisplay.\nA display\ncan be thought\nof as a stack of memory\nand graphics\nplanes\n(figure 2).\nMemory\nplanes\ncan be described\nas two-dimenslonal\nplanes\nwith each pixel in the memory\nplane having\nan intensity\nvalue\nthat\nranges\nfrom 0 through\n255.\nGraphics\nplanes\nare similiar\nin organization to memory\nplanes\nexcept\nthat each pixel of a graphics\nplane does\nnot hold a value between\n0 and 255 but rather\na value of either\n1 or 0\nrepresenting\neither\nan "on" or "off" state.\nGraphics\nused as an overlay\nto an already\ndisplayed\nimage and\nin a number of colors\ndepending\non the display.\n\nplanes\nare usually\nmay be displayed\n\nA look-up(LUT)\ntable allows\na user to alter or map the relationship\nbetween\nthe values\nin the memory\nplanes\nand the displayed\nintensities\n(figure\n3).\nA look-up\ntable can be thought\nof as a list of "from"\nand "to" translations\nsuch that each possible\ninput data value (0\nthrough\n255) is an index into a table of output\nintensity\nvalues.\nFor example,\nif the first entry in the LUT is I00, every pixel in the\nmemory\nthat has a value of 0 will be displayed\nas though\nit had an\nintensity\nof I00.\nThe original\nimage data remains\nunchanged\nbut is\ndisplayed\nwith different\nintensities.\nEach LUT has three components-one for the red band or component\nof the image,\none for the green\ncomponent,\nand one for the blue component.\n\n212\n\nThe\n\nfourth\n\ncharacteristic,\n\na pointing\n\ndevice,\n\nis\n\nusually\n\navailable\n\nwith a display\nand includes\nsuch devices\nas a trackball,\njoystick,\nor mouse.\nThese devices\nprovide capabilities\nsuch as cursor movement.\nMany of the LAS display\nmodules\nalso make use of the function\nbuttons\nfound on these devices.\nIll.\nIn\n\nModifications\n\ndesigning\n\nthat\n\nseveral\n\nand\n\nand\n\nAdditions\n\ndeveloping\n\nmodifications\n\nto\n\nthe LAS\nto the\n\nthe\n\nDisplay\n\ndisplay\n\nprototype\n\nManagement\n\nSystem\n\nmodules,\n\nit was\n\nDMS\n\nwe\n\nthat\n\nwere\n\ndiscovered\nworking\n\nwith were necessary\nto meet all the user-defined\nrequirements.\nThese\nchanges\nwere adopted\nby the Goddard\nSpace Flight\nCenter\nand have been\nincorporated\nin the DMS presently\nbeing distributed.\nOne such modification\ninvolved\nchanges\nto several\nof the DMS tables which\nstore device\nand image tracking\ninformation.\nMost of these modifications\nconsisted\nof moving\nfields\nfrom one table to another\nor adding\nfields\nto a table.\nThe first table that needed\nmodification\nwas the display\ndevice\ntable\nwhich holds device\nspecific\ninformation\n(figure\n4).\nFields\nwere added\nto store cursor\ninformation\nwhich includes\nthe active\ncursor\nnumber,\nthe cursor\nshape,\nsize, color and blink rate.\nA graphics\nplane mask\nwas also added to store the graphics\nplanes\nwhich are currently\non. The\ndisplay\nmemory\ntable which holds information\nabout each memory\nplane\nin a device\nwas modified\nnext (figure\n5).\nSeveral\nfields\nwere moved\nfrom this table to the image configuration\ntable.\nThis was to allow\neach defined image\nto have its own shift,\nzoom, memory\nwindow,\nand\nsource\nfile name rather\nthan linking\nthese attributes\nto a specific\nmemory\nplane.\nThe last table to be modified\nwas the image configuration table which holds information\nabout each defined\nimage (figure\n6).\nThe image configuration\ntable was renamed\nto the display\nparameter\ntable to clear up some user confusion\nand the image group information\nwas removed.\nA field was also added to keep track of LUT table information,\nto store a graphics\nactive record\nlist name, and to store the\nLAS file window\nand band numbers.\nThe\n\nsecond\n\nroutines\ndisplay\n\nmodification\n\ninvolved\n\ncaused\none of the\nmodule\ndevelopment.\n\ncoordinate\n\nconversions.\n\nThese\n\nlargest problems\nencountered\nin LAS\nThe LAS display\nmodules\nuse three\n\ndifferent\ncoordinate\nsystems\nto reference\nthe spatial\nlocations\nof\nthe image (figure\n7): (I) file coordinates\nwhich represent\nthe line\nand sample\nlocation\nfrom the upper-left\ncorner\nof the original\nLAS\nimage as it resides\non disk, (2) image coordinates\nwhich represent\nthe line and sample\nlocation\nfrom the upper-left\ncorner\nof the image\nas it resides\nin the memory\nplane, and (3) screen\ncoordinates\nwhich\nrepresent\nthe llne and sample location\nfrom the upper-left\ncorner\nof\nthe display\nscreen.\nBecause\nof the different\ncoordinate\nsystems,\nit\nwas necessary\nto write\ncoordinate\nconversion\nroutines.\nDifferences\nin dlsplay\norigins,\ndifferences\nin the\nzooming\nand shifting\nof memory planes,\ndevices\nreturn\ncursor\ncoordinates\nmade\nconversion\nroutines.\n\n213\n\nmanner\nthat dlsplays\nhandle\nand differences\nin how pointing\nit difficult\nto integrate\nthe\n\nThe methodof shift\n\ncalculation\n\nwas\n\nalso\n\nmodified\n\nand\n\nwas\n\nmade\n\na device\n\ndependent\ncalculation.\nOriginally,\nthe same shift calculation\nwas used\non all types of devices.\nThis caused\nproblems\nwith devices\nthat handled\nshifting\ndifferently.\nFor example,\nthe Raster\nTechnologies\nModel One/25\ndisplay\nonly allows\nshift values\nin multiples\nof 4 in the X direction\nand multiples\nof 2 in the Y direction\nbut the Deanza\nIP8500\ndisplay\ndoes\nnot impose this restriction.\nTherefore,\na different\nshift calculation\nroutine was needed\nfor each.\nIn\n\naddition\n\nto the\n\nmodifications\n\nmade\n\nwere also written\nto save, recall,\ntypes of image related\nfiles.\n\nto DMS,\n\nand\n\naccess\n\nseveral\n\nnew\n\ninformation\n\nutilities\nfrom\n\nthree\n\nThe first type, the display\nparameter\nfile, is a disk file that stores\nthe parameters\nnecessary\nto recreate\na view of an image.\nThis information includes\nthe zoom and shift factors\napplied\nto the image,\nthe LAS\nimage\ntable\n\nname, the LAS\ninformation.\n\nimage\n\nwindow,\n\nthe\n\nLAS\n\nimage\n\nbands,\n\nand\n\nThe second type of image related\nfiles are the graphics\nwhich\nare disk files that contain\ninformation\nnecessary\n\nthe\n\nlook-up\n\noverlay\nfiles\nto display\n\ngraphics\ndata along with descriptive\nattributes\nfor the data.\nThere\nare four types of graphics\noverlay\nfiles:\nfiles that store point data,\nfiles that store llne data, files that store polygon\ndata, and files\nthat store annotation\ndata.\nIn conjunction\nwith these modules,\nroutines\nwere also written\nto cllp the graphics\ndata and to automate\nlabel\nplacement.\nThe last image related\nfile is the active\nrecord\nllst.\nThis session\ntemporary\ndisk file contains\nthe name of the graphics\noverlay\nfile it\nreferences,\npointers\nto the currently\nactive\nrecords\nof the graphics\noverlay\nfile, and graphics\nplane masks to indicate\nwhich graphics\nplanes the data is currently\ndisplayed\nin.\nThese active\nrecord\nlists\nallow a user to display\nor manipulate\na defined\nsubset\nof graphics\noverlay data which helps speed processing\ntime.\nRoutines\noverlay\nIV.\nThe\n\nare\ndata\n\nalso\nto\n\nLAS Display\nLAS display\n\ncurrently\n\nformats\n\nin\n\ndevelopment\n\nuseable\n\nby\n\nother\n\nto convert\nsoftware\n\nthe\n\ngraphics\n\npackages.\n\nApplications\nmodules\n\nthemselves\n\nhave\n\nbeen\n\ngrouped\n\ninto\n\nfive\n\ncategories.\nColor display\nmodules\nprovide\nstatus\ninformation,\nallow allocation\nand deallocatlon\nof the display,\nallow loading\nand manipulation\nof images\nin the display\nmemory\nplanes,\nand saving\nimages\nfrom the memory\nplanes\nback to disk.\nMapping\nmodules\napply,\nsave, and restore\nintensity\nand pseudo\ncolor mappings.\nGraphics\noverlay modules\ncreate,\nsave, modify,\nand restore\ntext and linework\nand generate\nhistograms\nand tic marks.\nArithmetic\nmodules\ngenerate\noutput\nimages\nby performing\narithmetic,\nlogic,\nrotation,\nand convolution operations\non images,\nand cursor\nmodules\ndefine\nand turn a cursor\non and off and determine\ncursor\nlocations\nand intensity\nvalues.\n\n214\n\nThe\n\nfollowing\n\nis\n\nthe\n\nEROS\n\nCenter\n\nmodules\n\nData\nare\n\n11/780\n\nwith\n\nOne/25\n\nare\n\nAs\nare\n\nSome\n\nof\n\nfor\n\nthe\n\nEROS\n\nin\n\nMenlo\nfield\n\nthe\n\nLAS\n\na\n\nshort\n\na
